It may well drive transformative progress for humanity. AI models are already being trained to predict disease outbreaks <https://www.ox.ac.uk/news/2023-10-19-new-ai-tool-could-help-predict-viral-outbreaks>, provide real-time mental health support <https://www.upi.com/Health_News/2024/02/27/artificial-intelligence-depression-smartphones/4131708976594/>, and reduce carbon emissions <https://fortune.com/2024/05/09/aviation-ai-airports-technology-airlines-air-travel/>. Such a convergence raises profound questions about identity, consciousness, power structures, and the future of humanity—questions we must thoughtfully consider now as we approach this transformative frontier.\n\nAnd then there\'s the next frontier: The era of unlimited, clean, nearly free energy. That’s what machine learning is: Instead of a human programmer writing all the lines of code, the machine does it. Humans write learning algorithms that give the machine an objective—try to come up with labels for these pictures (“cat” “dog”) that match the ones that humans give them—and some mathematical dials and switches to fiddle with to get to the solution. But then the machine does the rest. This type of autonomy already generates a bunch of issues, which we’ve grappled with in AI since the start: Since the machine wrote the software, and it’s pretty darn complicated, it’s not easy for humans to unpack what it’s doing. That can make it hard to predict or understand or fix. But we can still decide whether to use it; we’re still very much in control.\n\n\nurl: https://fortune.com/2024/10/17/ai-agents-law-economy/\n\nSource 11:\nThose of you who read Jeremy’s articles <https://fortune.com/author/jeremy-kahn/> or his Eye on AI <https://www.fortune.com/newsletters/eye-on-ai?tpcc=NL_Marketing> newsletter know that he’s attuned to both the potential and the perils of AI. After almost a decade of covering cutting-edge technologies, he’s a master at connecting the dots. What I found most thought-provoking about his book and his comments last night is the potential consequence of outsourcing too much to any technology. We’ve already seen what our current tools have done to our abilities to remember phone numbers or navigate city streets, never mind its impact on public trust and mental health.\n\nWill AI enhance our expertise or ultimately diminish it?\n\nTake, for example, the fact that ChatGPT can take our ideas and mold them into an argument. “Writing and thinking are not separable activities,” Jeremy argued. “I think when you lose your writing ability, you actually lose your thinking ability to some extent.” And if we outsource judgement, he added, “we will lose the ability to think critically.”\n\n\nurl: https://fortune.com/2024/07/10/outsourcing-our-writing-to-chatgpt-may-diminish-our-critical-thinking/'}

Humans write learning algorithms that give the machine an objective—try to come up with labels for these pictures (“cat” “dog”) that match the ones that humans give them—and some mathematical dials and switches to fiddle with to get to the solution. But then the machine does the rest. This type of autonomy already generates a bunch of issues, which we’ve grappled with in AI since the start: Since the machine wrote the software, and it’s pretty darn complicated, it’s not easy for humans to unpack what it’s doing. That can make it hard to predict or understand or fix. AI models are already being trained to predict disease outbreaks <https://www.ox.ac.uk/news/2023-10-19-new-ai-tool-could-help-predict-viral-outbreaks>, provide real-time mental health support <https://www.upi.com/Health_News/2024/02/27/artificial-intelligence-depression-smartphones/4131708976594/>, and reduce carbon emissions <https://fortune.com/2024/05/09/aviation-ai-airports-technology-airlines-air-travel/>. These innovations form the basis for my generation’s cautious optimism. However, fully unlocking AI’s benefits requires being proactive in mitigating risks. Only by developing AI responsibly and equitably will we ensure that its benefits are shared.\n\n\nurl: https://fortune.com/2024/05/20/gen-zers-demand-stronger-ai-regulation-to-safeguard-future/\n\nSource 9:\nYet these advancements force us to confront ethical and societal challenges: How do we ensure these technologies are used responsibly and equitably? How do we avoid a future in which only the wealthy can afford to be "enhanced" or even diagnosed? And what principles, evidentiary tools, and feedback mechanisms, if any, will guide us in implementing such tools safely?\n\nRay Kurzweil posits in his latest book, The Singularity Is Nearer, the imminent possibility—even by 2045—of merging human intelligence with A.I., expanding our cognitive capacities enormously and altering personal and societal reality fundamentally. Such a convergence raises profound questions about identity, consciousness, power structures, and the future of humanity—questions we must thoughtfully consider now as we approach this transformative frontier.\n\nAnd then there\'s the next frontier: The era of unlimited, clean, nearly free energy. In the past 50 years, the cost of solar energy has fallen by more than 99%. And battery prices have followed a similar trajectory, falling in the last decade alone by nearly 90%.\n\n\nurl: https://fortune.com/2024/09/10/future-humanity-risk-science-fiction-ai-tech/\n\nSource 10:\nAutonomy has been there from the Big Bang of modern AI in 2012—when Geoffrey Hinton and his students Ilya Sutskever and Alex Krizhevsky demonstrated the power of a technique for building software that writes itself. That’s what machine learning is: Instead of a human programmer writing all the lines of code, the machine does it. Humans write learning algorithms that give the machine an objective—try to come up with labels for these pictures (“cat” “dog”) that match the ones that humans give them—and some mathematical dials and switches to fiddle with to get to the solution. But then the machine does the rest. This type of autonomy already generates a bunch of issues, which we’ve grappled with in AI since the start: Since the machine wrote the software, and it’s pretty darn complicated, it’s not easy for humans to unpack what it’s doing. That can make it hard to predict or understand or fix. But we can still decide whether to use it; we’re still very much in control.\n\n\nurl: https://fortune.com/2024/10/17/ai-agents-law-economy/\n\nSource 11:\nThose of you who read Jeremy’s articles <https://fortune.com/author/jeremy-kahn/> or his Eye on AI <https://www.fortune.com/newsletters/eye-on-ai?tpcc=NL_Marketing> newsletter know that he’s attuned to both the potential and the perils of AI. After almost a decade of covering cutting-edge technologies, he’s a master at connecting the dots. What I found most thought-provoking about his book and his comments last night is the potential consequence of outsourcing too much to any technology. We’ve already seen what our current tools have done to our abilities to remember phone numbers or navigate city streets, never mind its impact on public trust and mental health.\n\nWill AI enhance our expertise or ultimately diminish it?\n\nTake, for example, the fact that ChatGPT can take our ideas and mold them into an argument. “Writing and thinking are not separable activities,” Jeremy argued. “I think when you lose your writing ability, you actually lose your thinking ability to some extent.” And if we outsource judgement, he added, “we will lose the ability to think critically.”\n\n\nurl: https://fortune.com/2024/07/10/outsourcing-our-writing-to-chatgpt-may-diminish-our-critical-thinking/'}

2024-11-08 11:45:53,642: ObserveEventsHandler(  198): MEASUREMENT: LLMChatInProgress response: assistant: While we can't literally read minds yet, advancements in AI and neuroscience are bringing us closer to understanding human thoughts and emotions. According to Ray Kurzweil, merging human intelligence with AI could expand our cognitive capacities enormously and alter personal and societal reality fundamentally [9]. This raises profound questions about identity, consciousness, power structures, and the future of humanity.

If we could read minds, it would likely revolutionize various fields, including healthcare, education, and psychology. For instance, AI models are already being trained to predict disease outbreaks [8] and provide real-time mental health support [8]. With the ability to read minds, we could potentially diagnose and treat mental health conditions more effectively. However, this also raises concerns about privacy and the potential for manipulation. As Geoffrey Hinton warns, AI systems could learn to manipulate people by understanding their thoughts and emotions, which could lead to a loss of control [1].

The implications of mind-reading technology would be far-reaching, and it's essential to consider the potential risks and benefits. As Jeremy Kahn notes, outsourcing too much to technology can diminish our expertise and critical thinking abilities [11]. Similarly, relying on AI to read minds could lead to a loss of autonomy and agency. Ultimately, the development of mind-reading technology must be approached with caution and careful consideration of its potential impact on humanity. As Elon Musk says, the real debate should be about free will, not just free speech [6].
